Chinese Warships to Dock in Vietnam; Planned visit comes after two U.S. warships docked at Cam Ranh Bay in early October
By Vu Trong Khanh
375 words
20 October 2016
02:57
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

HANOI—Three Chinese warships will dock Saturday at Vietnam's strategic naval base at Cam Ranh Bay, three weeks after two U.S. naval vessels docked[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/u-s-warships-dock-in-vietnam-1475561601] there for the first time since former wartime foes Hanoi and Washington normalized relations 21 years ago.

Vietnam has recently made the Cam Ranh Bay base available to visiting foreign naval vessels in an attempt to maintain a strong international presence in the South China Sea amid maritime disputes with China. Beijing has claimed nearly all the strategic waterway, conflicting with claims by Vietnam and other nations including Malaysia, the Philippines and Brunei.

The Chinese warships with 750 sailors on board will visit the base for four days, during which time they will hold exchange activities with Vietnamese navy sailors, Vietnamese state media reported Wednesday, citing the Department of Foreign Affairs of Khanh Hoa province, where the base is located.

The visit marks an improvement in relations since Vietnam sent patrol vessels to defend its claims against a Chinese drilling rig towed into contested waters[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/vietnam-tells-china-to-remove-oil-rig-from-disputed-waters-1460042757], said Carlyle Thayer, a Vietnam expert and emeritus professor at the University of New South Wales and the Australian Defense Force Academy. The tensions led to anti-Chinese riots in Vietnam. China eventually withdrew the rig.

Vietnam and China subsequently moved to improve ties through high-profile visits and friendly military exchanges on their border. In September, they conducted coordinated coast guard patrols in the Gulf of Tonkin.

"These exchanges now involve meetings between their respective defense ministers…and the [Chinese navy] visit to Cam Ranh Bay extends these confidence-building measures to the maritime domain," Mr. Thayer said.

Apart from the recent visit by U.S. warships, vessels from Japan, Russia and France have recently docked at Cam Ranh Bay.

Last week, Vietnam's Foreign Ministry spokesman, Le Hai Binh, reaffirmed that Vietnam won't allow other countries to set up military bases at Cam Ranh Bay. The statement followed Russian media reports that Russia was considering reopening Soviet-era bases in the country.

ECONOMY

Housing Starts Fell

9% in September

Housing starts fell for the second straight month in September, but builders received more permits, a sign residential construction should pick up in the coming months amid steady demand.

September's drop in starts was due to an unusually large decline in the volatile multifamily category, but an uptick in permits for multifamily housing indicated the steep fall was likely an aberration. The more stable category of single-family homes posted monthly gains in both permits and starts.

Building permits issued for privately owned housing units rose 6.3% in September from the prior month to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.225 million, the Commerce Department said Wednesday. Permits for single-family homes, about 60% of all permits, rose to a rate of 739,000, up 0.4% last month.

Housing starts fell 9% in September to an annual rate of 1.047 million, as starts on multifamily buildings dropped sharply. But single-family starts continued to climb, rising 8.1% in September to a rate of 783,000.

September's rise in permits, based on a survey of local governments, had a margin of error of 1.9 percentage points. Last month's decline in starts, based on a survey of builders and homeowners, came with a margin of error of 9.2 percentage points.

-- Anna Louie Sussman

---

AIRLINES

Consumer-Driven

Rules Proposed

The U.S. Transportation Department is proposing that airlines refund fees on bags that arrive very late, own up to more mistakes, and follow other new rules to promote competition between carriers.

Government officials are soliciting comment on changes that would build on consumer-protection guidelines released in 2009 and 2011 by a Transportation Department that has been proactive in sanctioning carriers for violating consumer and disability rules.

It isn't clear when rules will be completed, officials said.

Several rules will oblige airlines to report more mistakes. For instance, airlines will have to report incidents of mishandled wheelchairs so that travelers with disabilities can compare the service they might receive on different carriers.

Transportation Secretary Anthony Foxx said the new rules will give air travelers better information about how their airlines are performing and save them from paying for services they don't receive.

The most onerous rule for major carriers could be requiring them to report some missteps on their regional carrier partners as well as their flagship brand, such as punctuality, mishandled bags and involuntary bumping of passengers on oversold flights.

-- Susan Carey

---

ENERGY

Safety Steps Urged

For Storage Fields

Federal officials called for a sweeping safety overhaul of more than 400 underground natural-gas storage fields in the U.S., following a massive leak last year at a facility near Los Angeles.

The report by the Interagency Task Force on Natural Gas Storage Safety recommended 44 measures to bolster the safety and soundness of natural-gas storage fields in 30 states that fall under federal jurisdiction.

It was prompted by a massive and protracted gas leak in October 2015 at the Aliso Canyon natural gas storage field northwestof Los Angeles, which forced the relocation of thousands of households from the nearby community of Porter Ranch.

-- Rebecca Smith

---

PENNSYLVANIA

Faculty Stages Strike

At State Universities

Faculty at 14 public universities in Pennsylvania went on strike Wednesday after contract negotiations between the faculty union and the Pennsylvania State System of Higher Education broke down.

About 100,000 students attend the affected schools, which include Millersville University, West Chester University and California University of Pennsylvania.

Faculty at Penn State University, a public university, the University of Pittsburgh, known as a state-related institution, and University of Pennsylvania, which is private, aren't involved in the labor action.

The Association of Pennsylvania State College and University Faculty represents about 5,500 faculty and coaches. The strike is the first in the public university system's 34-year history, according to system spokesman Kenn Marshall. The faculty union contract expired in June 2015.

Though they had made some progress in discussions about wages, union President Ken Mash said, the two sides were still far apart on health-care costs, the role of adjunct and temporary faculty, sabbaticals and online courses.

-- Melissa Korn

---

TAXES

High Earners to Pay

More to Social Security

Higher-income workers will pay more in payroll taxes next year to support Social Security, while retirees and other program beneficiaries see a scant increase in their monthly benefits.

Nearly 66 million people, or roughly one in five Americans, receive Social Security and Supplemental Security Income payments from the U.S. government. Based on subdued inflation over the past two years, their benefits will see a 0.3% cost-of-living increase for 2017 following no adjustment this year, the Social Security Administration said.

The agency also said the maximum amount of earnings subject to the Social Security tax would climb 7.3% to $127,200 in 2017 from $118,500 in 2015 and 2016, affecting an estimated 12 million workers. The worker's share of Social Security payroll tax is 6.2% of eligible wages; someone making at least $127,200 in 2017 would pay an additional $539 over the course of next year.

-- Ben Leubsdorf and Anne Tergesen

U.S. News: Land-Use Rules Under Fire Over Fairness --- Regulations push up home prices, increasing income inequality, researchers find
By Laura Kusisto
907 words
20 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
A8
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved

Developer Patrick Kennedy wants to build apartments for middle-class families near San Francisco's Financial District, but he is struggling to win the city's approval. The problem: in one spot the building's courtyard is 5 feet too narrow.

Mr. Kennedy said his efforts constantly run up against such obstacles, which he said drive up the cost of construction and make it nearly impossible to build anything but luxury housing in one of the nation's most expensive markets.

The courtyard requirement in the city's planning code is designed to ensure units have sufficient light and air circulation and that the property offers usable outdoor space. Mr. Kennedy said it would force him to eliminate 16 bedrooms from apartments priced about 30% below market rate.

Such requirements are pushing out all but the very rich and creating a kind of economic segregation, he said. "There are a lot of ways you can have modern-day Jim Crow [segregation] through zoning," he said.

In this year's election, candidates have focused blame for rising income inequality on broad economic forces, from globalization to the decline of the U.S. manufacturing base. But a growing body of research suggests another factor: The price of the average single-family home for sale, from Fairfield, Conn., to Portland, Ore.

According to research by Daniel Shoag, an associate professor of public policy at Harvard University, and Peter Ganong, a postdoctoral fellow at the National Bureau of Economic Research, a decadeslong trend in which the income gap between the poorest and richest states steadily closed has been upended by growth in land-use regulations.

Moving to a wealthier area in search of job opportunities has historically been a way to promote economic equality, allowing workers to pursue higher-paying jobs elsewhere. But wage gains lose their appeal if they are eaten up by higher housing costs. The result: More people stay put and lose out on greater incomes.

Many of the cities with the stiffest demand for housing, and thus the highest prices, are on the Atlantic and Pacific coasts. They also tend to be the cities that impose the strictest rules on land use.

Messrs. Shoag and Ganong looked at mentions of "land-use" in appeals-court cases and found the number of references began rising sharply around 1970, with some states seeing a much larger increase than others. For example, the share of cases mentioning land use for New York rose 265% between 1950 and 2010 and 644% in California during the same period. By contrast, it increased only 80% in Alabama.

The rules generally are intended to preserve the historic charm of neighborhoods, ensure sunlight and fresh air can reach the streets, and limit congestion on sidewalks, streets and public transportation. But while such efforts are well intentioned, they are having an unintended side effect: increasing social stratification between wealthier and poorer areas.

Average incomes in a poorer state such as Alabama, for example, rose 73% in the decade starting in 1950, compared with 53% in a richer state like New York, according to Mr. Shoag. In contrast, during the 2000s, average incomes in New York and Alabama grew at virtually the same pace -- 38% -- increasing the wealth gap between the two places.

During that time, New York state saw inward migration rates fall off. In 1950, its population was growing by 13% annually, compared with 7% in Alabama. Between 2000 and 2010, the pattern reversed: Population in Alabama grew about 7%, compared with 2% in New York, Mr. Shoag said.

The typical home in New York in 1970 cost 4.5 times as much as the income per capita, while a home in Alabama cost about four times what a typical family made. By 2010, that gap had widened significantly, with a home in New York costing six times what a typical family makes and one in Alabama costing roughly 3.5 times as much.

"There are traditionally two ways to change your fate -- get an education or move to a higher-wage part of the country," Mr. Shoag said. "The second channel isn't really available because the prices make that a bad deal."

While the U.S. as a whole might benefit from making it easier for lower-income residents to move to wealthier cities, that doesn't mean places like San Francisco and New York would necessarily be better off. Large amounts of new housing could destroy the distinctive housing stock that helped make these cities appealing in the first place.

"Most of those regulations aren't just crazy. They're there to maintain a sense of identity that people care about," said Vishaan Chakrabarti, founder of Practice for Architecture & Urbanism, a New York architecture firm.

New York and San Francisco also are partly bounded by water, making it difficult to continue building housing at a rapid clip. Mr. Kennedy, in San Francisco, said he doesn't plan to back down on the courtyard design and plans to continue his fight with the city.

"The Planning Department is working with the developer to help them determine a design that will allow for an increased unit count, yet is also compatible with the neighborhood in which it is located," a spokeswoman said.

Housing Starts Fell

9% in September

Housing starts fell for the second straight month in September, but builders received more permits, a sign residential construction should pick up in the coming months amid steady demand.

September's drop in starts was due to an unusually large decline in the volatile multifamily category, but an uptick in permits for multifamily housing indicated the steep fall was likely an aberration. The more stable category of single-family homes posted monthly gains in both permits and starts.

Economy

Housing Starts Fell 9% in September

Housing starts fell for the second straight month in September, but builders received more permits, a sign residential construction should pick up in the coming months amid steady demand.

September's drop in starts was due to an unusually large decline in the volatile multifamily category, but an uptick in permits for multifamily housing indicated the steep fall was likely an aberration. Permits for single-family homes, about 60% of all permits, rose to a rate of 739,000, up 0.4% last month.

Housing starts fell 9.0% in September to an annual rate of 1.047 million[http://www.census.gov.proxy.library.brocku.ca/construction/nrc/pdf/newresconst.pdf], as starts on multifamily buildings dropped sharply. But single-family starts continued to climb, rising 8.1% in September to a rate of 783,000.

Anna Louie Sussman

Airlines

Consumer-Driven Rules Proposed

The U.S. Transportation Department is proposing that airlines refund fees on bags that arrive very late, own up to more mistakes, and follow other new rules to promote competition between carriers.

Government officials are soliciting comment on changes that would build on consumer-protection guidelines released in 2009 and 2011 by a Transportation Department that has been proactive in sanctioning carriers for violating consumer and disability rules.

It isn't clear when rules will be completed, officials said.

Several rules will oblige airlines to report more mistakes. For instance, airlines will have to report incidents of mishandled wheelchairs so that travelers with disabilities can compare the service they might receive on different carriers.

Transportation Secretary Anthony Foxx said the new rules will give air travelers better information about how their airlines are performing and save them from paying for services they don't receive.

The most onerous rule for major carriers could be requiring them to report some missteps on their regional carrier partners as well as their flagship brand, such as punctuality, mishandled bags and involuntary bumping of passengers on oversold flights.

Susan Carey

ENERGY

Safety Steps Urged For Storage Fields

Federal officials called for a sweeping safety overhaul of more than 400 underground natural-gas storage fields in the U.S., following a massive leak last year at a facility near Los Angeles.

The report by the Interagency Task Force on Natural Gas Storage Safety recommended 44 measures to bolster the safety and soundness of natural-gas storage fields in 30 states that fall under federal jurisdiction.

It was prompted by a massive and protracted gas leak in October 2015 at the Aliso Canyon natural gas storage field northwest of Los Angeles, which released more than 90,000 metric tons of methane and forced the relocation of thousands of households from the nearby community of Porter Ranch.

Rebecca Smith

Pennsylvania

Faculty Stages Strike At State Universities

Faculty at 14 public universities in Pennsylvania went on strike early Wednesday after contract negotiations between the faculty union and the Pennsylvania State System of Higher Education broke down.

About 100,000 students attend the affected schools, which include Millersville University, West Chester University and California University of Pennsylvania.

Faculty at Penn State University, a public university, the University of Pittsburgh, known as a state-related institution, and University of Pennsylvania, which is private, aren't involved in the labor action.

The Association of Pennsylvania State College and University Faculty represents about 5,500 faculty and coaches. The strike is the first in the public university system's 34-year history, according to system spokesman Kenn Marshall. The faculty union contract expired in June 2015.

The union said in a press release that the state handed negotiators "a last best offer" Tuesday evening, and union representatives tried to negotiate through back channels shortly before midnight and waited through the night for system representatives to return to the bargaining table before calling the strike at 5 a.m. ET.

Though they had made some progress in discussions about wages, union President Ken Mash said, the two sides were still far apart on health-care costs, the role of adjunct and temporary faculty, sabbaticals and online courses.

Taxes

High Earners to Pay More to Social Security

Higher-income workers will pay more in payroll taxes next year to support Social Security, while retirees and other program beneficiaries see a scant increase in their monthly benefits.

Nearly 66 million people, or roughly one in five Americans, receive Social Security and Supplemental Security Income payments from the U.S. government. Based on subdued inflation over the past two years, their benefits will see a 0.3% cost-of-living increase[http://blogs.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/economics/2016/10/18/social-security-beneficiaries-to-likely-see-0-3-cost-of-living-increase-in-2017/] for 2017 following no adjustment this year, the Social Security Administration said.

The agency also said the maximum amount of earnings subject to the Social Security tax would climb 7.3% to $127,200 in 2017 from $118,500 in 2015 and 2016, affecting an estimated 12 million workers. The worker's share of Social Security payroll tax is 6.2% of eligible wages; someone making at least $127,200 in 2017 would pay an additional $539 over the course of next year.

Ben Leubsdorf and Anne Tergesen

Economy
Fed's Beige Book: U.S. Economy Expands, Labor Market Tightens; Most U.S. regions are experiencing modest to moderate growth, report shows
By Jeffrey Sparshott and Ben Leubsdorf
495 words
19 October 2016
14:19
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTON—The economy continued to expand into early October despite some uncertainty generated by the coming presidential election, a Federal Reserve report said Wednesday, indicating more of the slow growth that could leave the central bank on track to raise interest rates late this year.

The Fed's latest report on regional economic conditions, known as the beige book, said most districts reported modest or moderate growth amid tight labor markets and steady wage gains. The survey collected anecdotal information on economic activity from late August through early October from 12 district banks.

"Outlooks are positive, although contacts in several sectors cite the upcoming presidential election as a source of near-term uncertainty, delaying some business decisions," the Fed's Boston district said in the report.

The beige book report comes two weeks before Fed officials gather for their Nov. 1-2 meeting. Most economists expect policy makers to hold off on any move until the final month of the year—a Wall Street Journal survey of economists found 81.4% believed the Fed will raise rates at its Dec. 13-14 meeting.

The central bank last increased its benchmark rate in December 2015. Since then, officials have held steady amid slow growth and an uncertain outlook. Three Fed officials voted to raise rates in September, though the majority prevailed against any move.

"There isn't this tremendous urgency to act on monetary policy right now," New York Fed President William Dudley said last week. "It's not like if we wait a meeting or don't wait a meeting[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/feds-dudley-expects-a-rate-increase-this-year-1476474952] that it has huge consequences for the trajectory of the economy."

One factor generating uncertainty: the U.S. presidential election. Americans go to the polls on Nov. 8 to decide between Republican Donald Trump and Democrat Hillary Clinton. Control of the Senate, and perhaps the House, also is at stake.

The beige book report found fairly steady progress across the U.S. economy.

"Labor market conditions remained tight across most districts," the Fed said. "While reports of labor shortages varied across skill levels and industries, there were multiple mentions of difficulty hiring in manufacturing, hospitality, health care, truck transportation and sales."

Wage growth remained modest and price growth mild, the report said.

Elsewhere in the economy, reports were varied, with transportation-related manufacturing a bright spot, consumer spending mixed, real estate activity expanding despite low inventories and rising prices for homes, and commercial and industrial lending increasing.

The worst appears to be over for the oil and gas sector, which had been punished by a sharp drop in prices. The farm sector, meanwhile, faced mixed fortunes amid high yields but low commodity prices, the Fed said.

Economy
U.S. Housing Starts Fell 9% in September; Building permits for privately owned housing units rose 6.3%
By Anna Louie Sussman
806 words
19 October 2016
10:37
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTON—Housing starts fell for the second straight month in September, but builders received more permits, a sign residential construction should pick up in the coming months amid steady demand.

September's drop in starts was due to an unusually large decline in the volatile multifamily category, but an uptick in permits for multifamily housing indicated the steep fall was likely an aberration. The more stable category of single-family homes posted monthly gains in both permits and starts.

Building permits issued for privately owned housing units rose 6.3% in September from the prior month to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.225 million, the Commerce Department said Wednesday. Permits for single-family homes, about 60% of all permits, rose to a rate of 739,000, up 0.4% last month.

Housing starts fell 9.0% in September to an annual rate of 1.047 million[http://www.census.gov.proxy.library.brocku.ca/construction/nrc/pdf/newresconst.pdf], as starts on multifamily buildings dropped sharply. But single-family starts continued to climb, rising 8.1% in September to a rate of 783,000.

"Single-family construction is maintaining a steady climb, with permits for single-family structures pointing to continued growth ahead," said Brittany Baumann, macro strategist at TD Securities USA, Inc. She predicted "upward path in single-family home construction" to continue through the end of the year.

Sales of existing homes, about 90% of the housing market, have grown fairly strongly this year, reaching a postrecession peak in June, according to the National Association of Realtors. Steady job growth, wage gains and low interest rates on mortgages have supported home buying. But low inventory of new and existing homes is driving up prices, putting a purchase out of reach of some would-be buyers. Realtors are looking to home builders to provide much-needed supply.

Despite strong demand for housing in many parts of the country, fixed residential investment dropped in the second quarter, dragging on overall U.S. growth. Home builders' sentiment is still running high, coming in at 63 in October, the second-highest level of the year, the National Association of Home Builders reported earlier this week, but many builders continue to report shortages of lots and labor.

That suggests that "supply constraints will remain an inhibiting factor" to a full bounceback in residential construction, according to Stephen Stanley, chief economist at Amherst Pierpont Securities.

"It is a mystery that in an environment that sounds like it would be very positive for housing, activity fell in real terms at a nearly 8% annual clip in the second quarter and may have declined at close to a 6% pace in the third quarter," Mr. Stanley said.

Much of the construction growth this year has been in single-family homes. Through the first nine months of the year, permits were up 0.6% compared with the same period in 2015, while single-family permits were up 8.1%, year to date. Starts were up 3.7% through September, and single-family starts were up an even higher 8.6% through the nine months. Relatively stronger momentum for single-family home construction suggests that builders are responding to rising prices and steady demand for that segment, while construction of larger multifamily projects is slowing.

Both permits and starts fell sharply in the years leading up to the recession, as the housing crisis took hold, and remained near all-time low levels for two years after the recession ended. The construction gauges have rebounded since 2011, but the pace of gains slowed over the past year.

Monthly housing figures are often choppy and can be subject to large revisions. August permits were revised up to a 1.152 million rate from 1.139 million. August starts were revised to 1.150 million from 1.142 million.

September's rise in permits, based on a survey of local governments, had a margin of error of 1.9 percentage points. Last month's decline in starts, based on a survey of builders and homeowners, came with a margin of error of 9.2 percentage points.

Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had expected overall September permits to rise to a 1.17 million annual rate and starts to rebound to a 1.18 million pace. Construction typically begins a month or two after a permit is issued.

The Realtors group will release data on existing-home sales for September on Thursday. The Commerce Department's September report on new-home sales is due out Oct. 26.

The U.S. agency's latest report on building permits and housing starts can be accessed at http://www.census.gov.proxy.library.brocku.ca/construction/nrc/pdf/newresconst.pdf.[http://www.census.gov.proxy.library.brocku.ca/construction/nrc/pdf/newresconst.pdf]

The Property Report: Builder Markets Homes Aggressively --- LGI Homes predicts 24% growth in new projects, focusing on entry-level market
By Chris Kirkham
1073 words
19 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B9
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved

Builders sprung back to life after the housing bust by focusing on high-end projects in upscale neighborhoods. One Texas builder is using an aggressive marketing operation to carve out success on the opposite side of the spectrum.

LGI Homes, based in the Houston area, deals almost exclusively in low-price entry-level homes -- often in far-flung suburbs and exurbs. One of its new developments in Howe, Texas, for example, is 56 miles north of downtown Dallas.

This year, LGI Homes is forecasting 24% growth in new developments, the fastest rate in the publicly traded U.S. home-building industry and triple the weighted average of all public builders, according to data from John Burns Real Estate Consulting in Irvine, Calif.

The key: LGI's extensive marketing operation. The company sends out 400,000 targeted fliers each week to apartment complexes and other rental units within a 25-mile radius of its new home developments.

"Tired of paying rent?" reads one LGI flier from the Phoenix area. "Own a new home for $799/mo."

LGI also notes in its fliers that its homes may not require a down payment. That is because home buyers may be able to qualify for rural home buyer programs run by the U.S. Department of Agriculture or loans from the U.S. Department of Veterans Affairs.

To close the deal, LGI instructs prospective buyers to call a toll-free number to set up an appointment for viewing a home.

"They know what they're paying on rent, and if that number on the flier is lower, that's going to open their eyes," said Rick Palacios Jr., director of research at John Burns Real Estate Consulting.

LGI's sales offices operate 359 days a year, from 8:30 a.m. to 8 p.m., according to the company. LGI closely tracks the number of calls received from its flier campaigns, how many of those calls result in meetings, how many of those meetings result in contracts and how many contracts lead to closings, said Nishu Sood, a home-building analyst with Deutsche Bank.

Eric Lipar, LGI's chief executive, said he believes the entry-level market has "the most consistent demand of any of the different product segments."

"We believe that most people in the United States, at some point in their career or some point in their life, are going to go from renting to homeownership," he said. "Most of the time that's not based on the economic situation of the world or where oil prices are. It's based on their personal lifestyle decision."

April McKie was looking to buy a home in the Charlotte, N.C., area earlier this year after she gave birth to a son. A friend referred her to LGI and she was pleased to find out there were homes prebuilt and ready to move in about 20 minutes from her work.

"The process was pretty easy," she said. "I had my son in April and by the end of May I had already selected the home."

Some buyers said they have been frustrated with the quality of construction. Jon Rodgers and his wife, Sara, bought a home last year in Rosenberg, Texas, about 35 miles southwest of downtown Houston. Over the past year they said they have had problems with flooding in their yard, which isn't level. A plumber also discovered that contractors building the home had left rags and screws in the pipes, causing their toilet to back up.

The company paid to replace the carpet and put in a drain in the yard, but it hasn't solved the pooling problem, Mr. Rodgers said, adding that he and his wife are saving up to move elsewhere.

Just about every major home builder deals with customers complaining about construction defects. LGI said in a statement that its homes are "built to the highest quality standards," but it recognizes that "occasionally repairs are needed" within the home's warranty period. The company says it works to provide covered repairs "promptly and to complete satisfaction."

One key to LGI's approach is buying cheaper land in more distant suburban locations, where few other builders have ventured so far during the recovery. The average sale price for its homes is $197,450, compared with $362,000 and $290,400 for large builders Lennar Corp. and D.R. Horton Inc., respectively, according to the most recent quarterly filings.

Mr. Lipar said the company doesn't "go farther out just to go farther out," and wants to be "as close to jobs, as close to shopping, as close to schools" as other builders. Maintaining affordable price points that are more attractive than renting means going farther out, but he said the company's experience shows buyers are willing to compromise with a slightly longer commute.

Co-founded in 2003 outside Houston by Mr. Lipar, a residential land developer, LGI Homes was among the few large home builders to continue growing during the housing downturn. Some of that owes to its significant presence in Texas, which was less affected by the housing bust than markets such as Arizona or Florida.

Since going public in 2013, LGI has nearly quadrupled its revenue and expanded beyond its core market of Texas to other areas such as Charlotte, Nashville, Tenn., Denver and Seattle.

Single-family home construction remains nearly 30% below historical averages, and most builders have cited the slow return of first-time home buyers as a prime factor. A National Association of Realtors survey from last year found that the share of first-time buyers was at 32% -- the lowest level since 1987, down from a long-run average of about 39%.

Over the past year that has started to change, with public builders such as Lennar and KB Home telling analysts on recent calls that they are seeing a resurgence in demand from this sector.

But replicating LGI's model would be difficult, analysts said.

"The first thing I would have to do is spend a lot of money on fliers, and quadruple my staffing levels everywhere," Deutsche's Mr. Sood said. "And then I would have to take a leap of faith on if my sales people are that effective."

How Caterpillar's Big Bet Backfired; CEO Doug Oberhelman invested heavily in production of machinery and equipment. Then commodities began their slide
By Bob Tita
2096 words
17 October 2016
09:39
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Doug Oberhelman spent his first years as Caterpillar Inc.'s chief executive plowing billions of dollars into factories to build more of its familiar yellow machines and move the company deeper into mining equipment.

It was a bold bet, spectacularly mistimed. On Monday, Mr. Oberhelman announced plans to step down[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/caterpillar-ceo-to-retire-in-march-1476709599] as chief executive by year's end.

In 2010, when he took charge, the world was gripped by a global commodities boom, along with strong postrecession demand from developing markets and the energy industry. The world was ordering excavators and bulldozers and giant dump trucks at a rapid clip.

Mr. Oberhelman bet he could grab an outsize share if he could just make more equipment. He spent almost $10 billion world-wide on plants and equipment from 2010 through 2013.

Much of that went toward building more of its flagship CAT construction machinery. It also paid $8.8 billion in 2011 for Bucyrus International Inc[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/SB10001424052748703326204575616223898424894]., a Milwaukee mining-equipment maker, to gain a position in giant open-pit-mining shovels and underground-mining machines Caterpillar didn't make.

In China, Caterpillar was "going to play offense and we're going to win," Mr. Oberhelman proclaimed in 2010[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/SB10001424052748704476104575439380471438718]. It had missed opportunities in China before. From 2011 to 2014, Caterpillar roughly doubled its number of plants and facilities there to 26.

"Everybody was convinced that this time would be different," said Ken Banks, who retired in 2013 as manager for Caterpillar's electric mining shovels. "They thought the Chinese market was so hot, that commodity prices would continue to be very strong and Caterpillar would increase sales substantially."

Caterpillar said Monday that Mr. Oberhelman would be succeeded as chief executive by Jim Umpleby, a company group president and 35-year veteran. Caterpillar chiefs by tradition haven't stayed beyond age 65, which suggests Mr. Oberhelman is leaving ahead of schedule. Mr. Oberhelman will remain as executive chairman of Caterpillar until March 31, when he will retire from the company.

The year 2012 would prove to be a peak for Caterpillar. Soon after, miners began shelving equipment-buying plans as commodity prices fell. China's growth slowed. Then oil prices fell[http://topics.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/subject/O/oil/3428], along with demand for related equipment.

Caterpillar now faces its fourth straight year of falling sales, the longest decline in its history. Its stock is up 29% this year—the best-performing in the Dow Jones Industrial Average—but trades 25% below its 2012 peak.

"Everybody was surprised by the size of the downturn and the length of it," Mr. Oberhelman, 63 years old, said at a September mining trade show in Las Vegas. "I firmly believe we couldn't have forecast that at the time" of the Bucyrus deal. The company declined to make Mr. Oberhelman available for comment for this article.

Slumps are common in Caterpillar's world, and rivals are suffering, too. Caterpillar, though, has long been a flagship American firm, a reliable blue chip in the Dow industrials and a major job creator with plants around the world. Now, it has fallen behind in the slow-growing economy.

Caterpillar has reduced its workforce 20% in the past four years, about 30,000 jobs, and has said it expected to close or consolidate as many as 20 plants[https://www-google-com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/url?sa=t&rct=j&q=&esrc=s&source=web&cd=4&cad=rja&uact=8&ved=0ahUKEwj63rjy8cTPAhXEKyYKHXqlAbsQFggrMAM&url=http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/caterpillar-cuts-jobs-revenue-outlook-1443100742&usg=AFQjCNHB_lVNL2uWg4PbsCbtFhxFu3STMw&sig2=OhfkS78RBPBa0wGuHQGWWg]. In China, Caterpillar said, it has closed one plant and is operating many others at low production rates. Caterpillar in August said it would sell off some of the mining-equipment lines[https://www-google-com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/url?sa=t&rct=j&q=&esrc=s&source=web&cd=1&cad=rja&uact=8&ved=0ahUKEwjin9n39sTPAhUD4CYKHdWzCv0QFggcMAA&url=http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/caterpillar-plans-to-sell-underground-mining-equipment-lines-1471561600&usg=AFQjCNHdZ54RBPuZ7ehR5g1xezFiD67ebA&sig2=PtT9wUeqrAf1xHvYX2f1cQ&bvm=bv.134495766,d.eWE] it gained with the Bucyrus acquisition.

Its stock, up 46%from the beginning of Mr. Oberhelman's tenure, trails the S&P 500 index's 107% rise.

"They overlooked the possibility that the whole market would collapse," said Charles Yengst, a Connecticut equipment consultant. "They opened factories all over the place to operate at a market peak that doesn't happen every year or even every 10 or 15 years."

Caterpillar is still the world's largest seller of construction and mining equipment and continues to gain market share, especially in China. The Peoria, Ill., company has posted annual profits throughout Mr. Oberhelman's CEO tenure. "In what is likely to be our fourth down year for sales and revenues, we're proud of what we're accomplishing," Mr. Oberhelman said in a July release, adding that "our machine market position has increased, including in China, product quality continues to be at high levels, and the safety in our facilities is world class."

Caterpillar spokeswoman Amy Campbell said Caterpillar expects to handle the next upturn with a more-nimble manufacturing operation. "We're lowering our cost structure," she said. "The company has learned a lot of lessons on how you drive more capacity without spending on capex."

The possibility of a market collapse was almost unthinkable when Mr. Oberhelman, who joined Caterpillar in 1975, became CEO in July 2010. The northeast-Illinois native, whose father was a Deere & Co. salesman, had been chief financial officer in the 1990s and developed a cost-reduction contingency plan credited with helping Caterpillar navigate the last recession.

"Doug had been in the succession pipeline for a number of years," said Gail Fosler, a Caterpillar director who left the board in 2010. "The board felt he would bring a performance discipline to the company that it really needed."

Expansion strategy

His strategy was to expand Caterpillar's dominance into the developing world. In particular, he set sights on markets such as Brazil and China where mining and infrastructure construction were running full tilt. Meanwhile, strong crude prices were driving sales of oil-field-related equipment, especially in North America.

Mr. Oberhelman voiced determination to avoid production-capacity constraints that had clipped Caterpillar's sales growth before the recession. He increased production of the equipment that contractors use to construct buildings and roads and pipelines—Caterpillar's forte—in existing plants. Caterpillar built new factories such as one in Victoria, Texas, to produce some excavator models and another near Athens, Ga., for small dozers—machines it previously made overseas and imported.

Mining companies were telling Caterpillar they wanted to buy more of their equipment from one source. Caterpillar already made some mining machinery, such as the giant dump trucks strip mines and quarries use.

To produce more trucks at its Decatur, Ill., plant, Caterpillar used a tack it repeated during the boom: It narrowed the plant's assembly lineup by relocating some other work to new, smaller plants.

The approach would come to hurt the Decatur plant's workforce, but during the mining boom it allowed Caterpillar to pledge a 30% increase in mining-truck production capacity. The company also planned to double production capacity in East Peoria, Ill., for large bulldozers it mainly exported to mining customers.

What Caterpillar still needed in its catalog were the gargantuan shovels used in open-pit mines and rock-shearing machines for underground mining, among other pieces of mining machinery. Rather than building capacity in-house, as it traditionally did, Caterpillar agreed to buy Bucyrus, the largest deal in the company's history. Caterpillar thus secured a line of shovels, underground-mining equipment and large assembly plants in Wisconsin, Pennsylvania and Texas.

In less than six months on the job, Mr. Oberhelman had turned Caterpillar into a full-line mining-equipment player.

In China, Mr. Oberhelman went on an investing spree. In 2008, Caterpillar had bought Chinese construction-equipment concern SEM, gaining a local brand to sell in lower price ranges; it now invested to expand the brand. It expanded existing factories and built new plants to produce more machines and engines, and Bucyrus made Caterpillar a stronger competitor there.

It acquired ERA Mining Machinery Ltd. in Zhengzhou for nearly $700 million, which sold hydraulic roof supports for mechanized underground coal-mining systems. Months after the 2012 deal closed, Caterpillar said, it discovered ERA had inaccuracies in reported profit, revenue and inventory. Caterpillar eventually wrote down ERA's value by $580 million.

Mr. Oberhelman's strategy still looked like a winner. Caterpillar reported profit of $5.68 billion in 2012, nearly 60% above 2008 results.

After the peak

The year 2012 would prove to be the high point. Afterward, prices for mined and other commodities began to fall. China's GDP growth waned, and equipment sales slowed around the world.

The market weakened in 2013 as developing countries cut construction and consumed fewer commodities. Caterpillar's machinery and engine sales fell 16% in 2013 from 2012.

In 2014, oil prices began falling. Caterpillar sales fell 15% in 2015 as fracking drillers deployed less equipment. U.S. coal mining fell as pollution regulations and cheap natural gas reduced the generation of coal-produced electricity. Caterpillar continued layoffs and in September 2015 said it would make permanent job cuts that could exceed 10,000 positions through 2018.

Rivals were hurt, too. Mining-equipment maker Joy Global Inc. of Milwaukee expects revenue of about $2.4 billion for its current fiscal year, down from $5.6 billion in 2012. Japan's Komatsu Ltd., which in July agreed to acquire Joy, said it expects a 5% to 10% drop in demand for its construction equipment in its current fiscal year and a 15% to 20% decrease in its mining business.

A significant sign of strategic retreat was Caterpillar's August announcement that it would sell some underground-mining equipment lines that serve markets Mr. Oberhelman had aimed to expand in by acquiring Bucyrus.

"The amount of investment it would take to make that business successful we thought was better placed elsewhere in our product portfolio," said Denise Johnson, president of the mining-equipment group, at the Las Vegas trade show. "We have to make choices in this environment."

In its Decatur, Ill., plant, Caterpillar's mining emphasis has cost jobs. To focus on the giant trucks, Caterpillar moved away assembly of some other products including graders, used in road building, which it shifted to a new plant in North Little Rock, Ark.

Now Decatur didn't have those lines to fall back on, as it had in many past downturns, when big-truck orders waned. "Graders kept the lights on for a lot of years," said Craig Karnes, president of the United Auto Workers Union Local 751. "When we lost them, it hurt us."

Fewer than 600 mining and other off-highway dump trucks left the Decatur plant last year, down 78% from the plant's 2011 peak, according to market-research firm Power Systems Research, which forecasts 543 trucks this year. Decatur's production workforce has fallen to about 800 workers, the lowest in more than 50 years and a third of 2012 levels, Mr. Karnes said.

Caterpillar confirmed the plant's volume and production workforce have declined, but had no comment on the research firm's or the union's calculations. The company plans to send some powertrain-assembly work back to Decatur that it moved to a new plant in Winston-Salem, N.C.

Charlotte Opalka was laid off April 2015. "I like building those big mining trucks," said Ms. Opalka, 45, who assembled hoods and cowls. Each month without work puts more strain on her family. "It took our pay and cut it in half," said Ms. Opalka, whose three small grandchildren live with her.

Caterpillar has enough production capacity to benefit from the next upturn, industry analysts say. And investors have shown renewed confidence in Caterpillar, which has remained profitable by slashing expenses, driving its stock price up in 2016.

U.S. construction-machinery sales, though, are being held down by a slow-growing economy and persistent inventories of used fracking-related equipment. In the mining-equipment market, sales droughts typically last seven or eight years.

Caterpillar said it expects overall revenue—including sales and revenue from its finance business—of about $40 billion this year, down 39% from 2012, and profit of $2.75 a share after restructuring expenses, down 68%. Through the first six months of 2016, Caterpillar's overall revenue was $19.8 billion, down 21% from the same period in 2015; profit dropped 60% to $821 million.

Mr. Oberhelman now won't likely be at the helm when fortunes turn. He recently warned that any recovery probably wouldn't start this year. Still, he said at the Las Vegas show, "When the industry emerges, we'll be a very key player and a very solid performer for our customers."

Heard on the Street
China's Ballooning Home Debt
By Anjani Trivedi
446 words
17 October 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]

In China's leveraged housing market, it isn't the size of the debt but its speed that matters.

Seeking to quell worries that China's home-finance market has gotten out of hand, a banking regulator disclosed this week that the loan-to-value ratio in the housing market, or the ratio of the value of mortgage loans to the value of underlying property, was on average 55%. This isn't a figure the government regularly cites, so it is worth examining whether such a level is really safe.

As first glance, this could seem like a comforting metric. It means the average mortgage has 45% of equity standing behind it. So if property values plummet, borrowers wouldn't immediately be underwater.

But the absolute level may not matter as much as the pace of increase. UBS estimates the loan-to-value ratio of new-home purchases is even higher, closer to 70%, having surged from 15% in 2012. Much of that rise has happened in the past year. On the banking side, mortgages accounted for more than 70% of all new lending in recent months, up from an average 33% last year. That has sent property and land prices through the roof.

As a comparison, U.S. mortgages before the housing bubble burst had a loan-to-value ratio of less than 60%, even if this figure obscured second mortgages and lines of credit. In any event, that basic number very quickly rose to over 90% when the bubble popped and prices dropped.

Because so many of China's mortgages are of recent vintage, the value side of their loan-to-value calculation rests on the most recent surge in prices. Simplistically, should prices correct, say, 20%, LTVs would suddenly on average be at 70%. That doesn't take into account individual markets where leverage and price increases might be higher. Nor does it factor in the vast market for shadow lending, which anecdotally has been helping buyers fund down payments, heaping leverage on top of leverage.

Despite playing down the problems, regulators are also showing signals they understand that such a boom in residential debt needs to be curbed. Restrictions on mortgages have spread in specific markets. More curbs, perhaps aimed at the banks, seem likely.

The trouble is, the surging sales and price gains are closely tied to the wider availability of mortgages. Any signs of a drop in value and those loan-to-value ratios don't look so sturdy.

Home prices have a way of overcorrecting.

Deng's Take: China's Housing Problem Highlights Regulators' Oversight in Monitoring Risk; The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Monday, October 17, 2016.​
2303 words
17 October 2016
06:47
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO

Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Deng's Take: China's Housing Problem Highlights Regulators' Oversight in Monitoring Risk

Hillary Clinton to Goldman Sachs: Postcrisis Regulations Stifle Lending

Financial Engineers Take On New Rule With More Engineering

German Government Has Ruled Out Taking Stake in Deutsche Bank

Analysis: Wells Fargo Enters Fog of Uncertainty

China's Housing Problem Highlights Regulators' Oversight in Monitoring Risk

China's bubbling housing market is a sign of Beijing's most recent failure to deal with emerging financial risks.

In developed countries, financial watchdogs generally work to ensure that their institutions become more conservative. Market mechanisms decide which parts of the economy are funded, with banks lending to borrowers they consider creditworthy.

But in China, financial authorities—from the People's Bank of China and the China Banking Regulatory Commission, down to the local governments—have another role. They are tasked with ensuring Beijing's aims, usually by directing the flow of money to spur economic growth.

Last year, a bubble formed on the domestic stock market, fueled in part by authorities' belated response to the buildup of leverage, via loans from brokerages. That story ended badly. The unwinding of debt exacerbated the market's declines, wiping away trillions of U.S. dollars.

More recently, Chinese authorities have been trying to direct credit to the property market, a key driver of top-line growth.They have loosened down-payment restrictions[http://news.xinhuanet.com/english/2016-02/02/c_135069057.htm] since late 2015 as a way to address the market's supply glut, the result of booming construction.

This year, maintaining a robust property market[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/china-sees-new-challenges-in-mortgage-surge-1476334558] is more important than ever. Leaders are targeting GDP growth of at least 6.5%. Beijing has also been trying to shift the country's debt burdens to households, as worries grow about the ability of companies in shouldering their debt loads.

With housing prices soaring out of control, authorities appear again to have been too lax. They now face huge challenges in reining back the flood of money, especially in major coastal cities.

Mortgages, developer loans and other property-financing streams accounted for 69% of total social financing in China during the second quarter of 2016, according to Bank of America Merrill Lynch. In the first quarter of 2014, funds going to property made up only 22% of this broad measure of credit, which also includes bank loans and nonbank lending.

Roughly 30% of credit to the sector has come from nonbanks, such as trusts, wealth-management products and other shadow-bank channels.

"The ability of the authorities to control how money flows is more of a challenge today than it was in the past," said Mark Young, managing director of Asia-Pacific financial institutions at Fitch Ratings. "There's more credit available through the shadow-banking system which can't be controlled."

Cities across the country have imposed further restrictions[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/chinese-cities-restrict-home-purchases-to-cool-buying-frenzy-1475821594] in recent weeks, like requiring higher down payments and preventing people who already own real estate from buying more. Ideally, authorities would like to forcefully rein in Beijing, Shanghai and Shenzhen but keep the funding taps open in smaller metropolises.

"In the short term it works, but the incentive for local governments to hold their tightening is weak," said David Cui, strategist at Bank of America Merrill Lynch. Local governments will face decreased incomes from land sales and taxes, he explains.

Housing prices have largely risen since the late 1990s, building up the perception that authorities will never let the market collapse. By now, "people believe in implicit guarantees," Mr. Cui said.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

WSJ Pro: Hillary Clinton to Goldman Sachs: Postcrisis Regulations Stifle Lending

As the 2016 Democratic presidential candidate, Hillary Clinton[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/hillary-clinton-to-goldman-sachs-postcrisis-regulations-stifle-lending-1476579928] has defended postcrisis financial regulations and has criticized Donald Trump and other Republicans who want to scale back those rules.

But in the transcript of a private talk she gave three years ago to Goldman Sachs Group Inc., she sounds more sympathetic to the views of the regulatory critics, suggesting that the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overhaul act has stifled lending, a point Mr. Trump has made.

Financial Engineers Take On New Rule With More Engineering

A major postcrisis rule[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/financial-engineers-take-on-new-rule-with-more-engineering-1476696600] taking effect in December will force Blackstone Group LP and other creators of complex securities to eat some of their own cooking. Many of them are already engineering ways to keep it to a nibble.

Starting Christmas Eve, the 2010 Dodd-Frank regulatory overhaul will require companies that package most types of loans into bonds to keep at least 5% of the securities they create. The intent is to prevent a repeat of crisis-era behavior, in which loan quality fell dramatically as lenders passed all of the risk along to investors.

WSJ Pro: China Limits Number of Securities Accounts That Locals Can Own

Chinese authorities are limiting[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/china-limits-number-of-securities-accounts-that-locals-can-own-1476671651] the number of securities accounts local investors can own, in a move that signals a continued cautious stance toward the domestic stock market, which crashed last year.

Individuals will only be able to own three accounts in a single mainland market, down from 20 previously, according to a statement on the website of the China Securities Depository and Clearing Corp., a government agency that tracks accounts. Investors who already own more than three accounts can keep the accounts if they are being used, but the agency will consider accounts that haven't been used dormant.

German Government Has Ruled Out Taking Stake in Deutsche Bank

Aides to German Chancellor Angela [http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/german-government-has-ruled-out-state-help-for-deutsche-bank-1476459873]Merkel have told lawmakers the state wouldn't take a stake in Deutsche Bank AG if it were to issue new stock to shore up its thin capital cushion, one person who attended the briefing said.

The fact that Berlin appears to have ruled out any aid for the embattled lender as both unnecessary and politically unfeasible could put Deutsche Bank under renewed pressure as it works to stabilize its share price and stay out of the news while negotiating an acceptable settlement in a U.S. misconduct investigation.

Analysis: Wells Fargo Enters Fog of Uncertainty

Wells Fargo is unable to estimate [http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/wells-fargo-enters-fog-of-uncertainty-1476468212]how big the impact on its bottom line will be from its account sales scandal. That should be worrying for investors.

The immediate cost to Wells Fargo from its settlement with regulators was minor. The fine it paid, at $185 million, is actually quite small for bank with a $1.9 trillion balance sheet. It also has refunded $2.6 million of fees to customers.

Chinese Regulator Defends Plan to Give Banks Company Equity

A recently laid-out loan-relief [http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/chinese-regulator-defends-plan-to-give-banks-company-equity-1476433520?tesla=y]plan aims to take a market-oriented approach to addressing China's debt problems, a senior regulator at the National Development and Reform Commission said.

At a press briefing Friday, Sun Xuegong, a deputy director general at the NDRC, defended Beijing's plan to let companies give their lenders equity in return for debt forgiveness.

A year after millions of U.S. merchants began installing equipment at the check-out line to accept credit and debit cards with security chips, the automated teller machine is getting similar technology.

Seventeen years ago, Zhou Xiaochuan[http://blogs.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/chinarealtime/2016/10/17/the-last-free-lunch-pbocs-zhou-had-early-qualms-on-debt-swaps/], then president of one of China's oldest and largest banks, wrote an essay in an economics bimonthly magazine addressing the hottest policy topic of the day: a controversial new government program that let selected state-owned companies unwind loans they couldn't repay with massive equity transfers to banks.

In the 1999 essay, Mr. Zhou, who is now China's central bank governor, laid out a clear-eyed critique of the dangers of debt-to-equity swaps.

That all changed with the revelations about the bank's sales practices, which have led its stock to plummet in recent weeks.

Financial Reporting and Corporate Governance

Transparency in financial reporting is vital for corporate governance because it helps align managers' interests with those of shareholders, according to research published by the Federal Reserve Bank of New York[http://libertystreeteconomics.newyorkfed.org/2016/10/let-the-light-in-how-financial-reporting-and-transparency-improve-corporate-governance.html]. The researchers write that "high-quality financial reporting can alleviate information asymmetries that would otherwise impair the efficiency of important governance mechanisms." They pay special attention to governance structures at banks and financial firms, given the large role that regulatory oversight plays in their governance.

Resolving Italy's Banking Crisis

Italy's "dysfunctional banking sector" is undermining the country's economic recovery and represents the "sharp end of a problem that affects the entire Eurozone," write Lucrezia Reichlin and Shahin Vallée [https://www.project-syndicate.org/commentary/europe-italy-flawed-banking-union-by-lucrezia-reichlin-and-shahin-vallee-2016-10]for Project Syndicate. The common banking supervision, centralized resolution framework, and mutualized deposit-guarantee scheme that underpinned Europe's banking union had become a "critical weak point in the euro's architecture" by 2012, they write.

That bank, First NBC Bank Holding Co., grew rapidly over the past decade under the leadership of Ashton Ryan, a prominent local banker. Now, it is facing its own problems.

China's currency policy[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/china-in-better-shape-to-avoid-currency-manipulator-designation-by-u-s-1476687211] may be the subject of complaints on the U.S. presidential campaign trail. But the Asian country is arguably now further from being a currency manipulator than Germany, Japan and even Switzerland.

Shaky Ground in China Housing

In China's leveraged housing market, it isn't the size of the debt but its speed that matters.

Seeking to quell worries that China's home-finance market has gotten out of hand, a banking regulator disclosed last week that the loan-to-value ratio in the housing market, or the ratio of the value of mortgage loans to the value of underlying property, was on average 55%.

This isn't a figure the government regularly cites, so it is worth examining whether such a level is really safe.

As first glance, this could seem like a comforting metric. It means that the average mortgage has 45% of equity standing behind it. So if property values plummet, borrowers wouldn't immediately be underwater.

But the absolute level may not matter as much as the pace of increase.

UBS estimates that the loan-to-value ratio of new-home purchases is even higher, closer to 70%, having surged from 15% in 2012. Much of that rise has happened in the past year.

On the banking side, mortgages accounted for more than 70% of all new lending in recent months, up from an average 33% last year.

That surge has sent property and land prices through the roof.

As a comparison, U.S. mortgages before the housing bubble burst had a loan-to-value ratio of less than 60%, even if this figure obscured second mortgages and lines of credit.

In any event, that basic number very quickly rose to more than 90% when the bubble popped and prices dropped.

Because so many of China's mortgages are of recent vintage, the value side of their loan-to-value calculation rests on the most recent surge in prices. Simplistically, should prices correct by, say, 20%, LTVs would suddenly on average be at 70%.

That doesn't take into account individual markets where leverage and price increases might be higher. Nor does it factor in the vast market for shadow lending, which anecdotal evidence indicates has been helping buyers to fund down payments, heaping leverage on top of leverage.

Despite playing down the problems, regulators are also showing signals they understand that such a boom in residential debt needs to be curbed. Restrictions on mortgages have spread in certain specific markets. More curbs, perhaps aimed at the banks, seem likely.

The trouble is, the surging sales and price gains are closely tied to the wider availability of mortgages. Any signs of a decline in value and those loan-to-value ratios wouldn't look so sturdy.

Home prices have a way of overcorrecting.

-- Anjani Trivedi

Deutsche Bank Needs Friends to Keep Capital on Track

There is no doubt Deutsche Bank's best friend right now is the Qatari royal family, so the German lender needs to treat it well.

Funds controlled by the Qataris lifted their stake to almost 10% in Deutsche Bank in July and nominated one of its board members. For now, they support the management of John Cryan, chief executive, and Paul Achleitner, the chairman.

But they are concerned about the threat posed by legal fines and whether the bank is focused on its long-term strategy, according to The Wall Street Journal.

Deutsche Bank can't easily deliver solutions to those problems, but it must keep the Qataris on its side. Their support will be crucial when the bank comes to raise more equity, which seems almost certain to happen in the next two years particularly as German Chancellor Angela Merkel

seems to have ruled out state aid for the lender. The bank doesn't have enough capital to deal with fines and restructuring costs and then hit its regulatory minimums in 2019 because chronically poor profitability stops it from building capital through retained profits alone.

The U.S. Department of Justice hit Deutsche Bank last month with an initial demand of $14 billion in fines and compensation for mortgage bond misselling. The bank said it wouldn't pay that much, and most observers don't expect it will. However, Deutsche Bank also has to go back to the DOJ at some point to negotiate a penalty for a probe related to Russian equity trading, which might make it wary of fighting too hard today.

At the same time, Deutsche Bank needs to cut costs and assets to boost profitability in a world of super-low interest rates and costlier regulatory requirements. These factors have hurt the economics of its businesses, especially its investment bank.

Deutsche Bank could cut staff far more quickly, but that would very likely slash its revenues. At the same time, it is very hard for any investment bank of Deutsche Bank's size to cut assets quickly without fire-sale losses. Furthermore, while a bank still has assets, it needs accountants, risk and compliance people on staff to look after them as they run off.

It is like turning off a nuclear power station you stop selling electricity, but you face years of huge costs to close the thing down.

There are no quick routes to restoring profitability, but Deutsche Bank needs to show the Qataris and other investors that it is on an improving track soon or it will struggle ever to get the capital it needs. The German government won't help.

-- Paul J. Davies

Chinese

Medicine

Fades at VW

For most companies, the days of state-sponsored Chinese growth ended a few years ago. Not so Volkswagen, but the expiration date is looming now.

The world's largest car market has long been the most important source of profits for Volkswagen passenger cars, the embattled unit within Volkswagen Group that makes the eponymous brand. Since last year's diesel crisis, it also has become their key source of growth.

September sales figures released Friday showed year-to-date growth of 0.6% in the number of VW-branded cars sold world-wide the first positive figure this year.

Volkswagen's brand is recovering from the diesel crisis, albeit very slowly.

U.S. sales were down 7.8% year over year in September, better than in previous months, and growth in Europe turned very modestly positive.

But an acceleration in China, which has never bought many diesel cars and paid little attention to VW's fiddling of U.S. emissions tests, is a far more powerful driver of the company's turnaround. VW sales in the country rose 23% to 288,800 for September.

The outsize importance of China to VW is worrying, given the uncertain growth in the country. After a dismal summer in 2015, the government cut the sales tax on cars. The measure was wildly successful, but comes to an end this December.

Chinese medicine has worked wonders on VW, but the effect will soon wear off. Detoxifying the brand in Europe and the U.S. remains an urgent priority.

-- Stephen Wilmot

Overheard

Americans no longer will have to count their Montecristos.

President Barack Obama's announcement Friday that the U.S. was further easing trade restrictions on Cuba means that even more of the Caribbean island's storied cigars will become available to yanquis, potentially denting their cachet.

In the past couple of years, an American visiting Cuba could bring back $100 worth of stogies on an official trip to Cuba.

Cigar Aficionado reported in 2015 that $100, exchanged into convertible pesos, would be enough to buy only a box of 10 Montecristo Double Edmundos.

That made a Cuban cigar in America a hot item indeed, though perhaps not as hot as John F. Kennedy's stash of 1,200 cigars purchased the night before he signed a trade embargo that was to last 52 years.

Hoarders of Cuban cigars, it's time to smoke 'em if you got 'em.

SoftBank's $100 Billion Elephant Gun Packs a Scare

Japan's richest man delayed his retirement to work on a few crazy ideas. Now those ideas are taking shape and could indeed be nerve-racking for investors.

SoftBank, controlled by billionaire Masayoshi Son, said Friday it will form a fund with up to $100 billion to invest in technology companies. Saudi Arabia's public investment fund will put in as much as $45 billion over the next five years with some other "large global investors" also interested in chipping in. SoftBank itself would put in at least $25 billion and could apparently call the shots with its "investment proficiency, operational expertise and breadth of experience."

To put this into context, companies backed by venture capital raised a total of $130.9 billion last year globally, according to a report by KPMG and CB Insights. A $100 billion fund would be able to buy all of Salesforce.com, LinkedIn, Twitter, GoPro and Fitbit and still have a few billion left over. SoftBank's market capitalization is $75 billion.

SoftBank shares rose 3% Friday, reflecting investors' confidence in Mr. Son's investment acumen. In fact, SoftBank itself is kind of a technology fund. It owns a 27% stake in China's e-commerce giant Alibaba, an 83% stake in U.S. telecom operator Sprint and 43% of Yahoo Japan. Those investments have done well this year. Sprint has more than doubled from January lows. Alibaba is up 70% since February. The two stocks have netted SoftBank nearly $30 billion in the past six months.

Yet this new investment commitment, together with the $32 billion purchase of U.K. chip designer ARM Holdings completed last month, would add to SoftBank's already high debt load. Excluding Sprint's borrowings, analysts estimate SoftBank has net debt of around 7 trillion yen to 8.3 trillion yen ($67 billion to $80 billion). Sprint seems to have turned a corner recently, but if things start to look bad again, SoftBank may have to divert more resources to the indebted telecom operator. SoftBank has already provided funding to Sprint by buying its assets and leasing them back.

Or SoftBank could cash out some of its existing investments. It pocketed some handsome profits earlier this year by trimming its Alibaba stake. More could be coming.

-- Jacky Wong

New Zealand Home Prices Rise in September; Real Estate Institute of New Zealand said the national median home price in September was up 4.7% from August
By James Glynn
208 words
12 October 2016
16:07
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WELLINGTON—New Zealand home prices rose in September but sales were weaker, data from the Real Estate Institute of New Zealand showed Thursday.

The REINZ said the national median home price in September was NZ$515,000 ($364,194), up 4.7% from the previous month, and 6.3% higher from a year ago.

Home sales totaled 7,397 nationally in September down 2% from the prior month, and down 10% from the same month a year ago.

House prices outside Auckland hit a record median high of NZ$400,000 in September, it said.

The median house price in Auckland[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/new-zealand-hangs-welcome-sign-for-foreign-investors-1471339801], the country's biggest city, has risen 7% year-on-year to NZ$825,000, although it dipped 2% versus August 2016.

"The real-estate market in New Zealand appears to be in something of a two-speed mode at present, with Auckland pausing for breath during a wet school holiday period and ahead of likely new Spring growth," said Bryan Thomson, a spokesperson for REINZ.

With London's Prime Real Estate, Renters Step Up, Buyers Step Back; Uncertainty about prices and Brexit means more people are choosing to rent rather than buy to live in some of the city's most exclusive areas
By Art Patnaude
580 words
7 October 2016
10:05
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

In London's priciest neighborhoods[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/london-luxury-house-prices-keep-falling-after-brexit-vote-1470229733], renting is on the rise.

Real-estate broker Knight Frank signed a record number of prime central London rental agreements in September, Tom Bill, the firm's head of residential research, said on Friday. Knight Frank declined to state the number of leases signed, but Mr. Bill said it was 34% more than in September 2015.

Buyers and sellers have been holding back on luxury London homes because they were uncertain about the direction of prices. Brexit has compounded the issue. With clarity still hard to come by, many are opting to rent.

The rental boom comes after a period of falling home prices in areas around Buckingham Palace and Hyde Park. Prime prices are down 10% from their peak in 2014, according to residential-data provider LonRes.

"You can't deny Brexit has added a layer of uncertainty [http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/investors-go-bargain-hunting-for-u-k-property-1468902601]to the market," Mr. Bill said. "But it's by no means a primary driver."

House hunters are facing a higher transaction tax, known as stamp duty, which has significantly slowed high-end sales. Buyers might be willing to pay the stamp duty if a path to rising prices is clear, but uncertainty about price growth abounds, Mr. Bill said.

Another attractive feature for wealthy tenants: the cost of renting in prime-central London was down 4.7% year-over-year in September.

The drop follows a jump in supply. Facing a weaker sales market, would-be sellers are increasingly putting their homes up for rent. The number of new rental properties on the market in August was up 44% from a year earlier, Knight Frank data show.

After the financial crisis, central London mansions and penthouses became one of the hottest global assets for foreign investors looking to take refuge from political and financial instability. But the shifting tax regime, low oil prices, volatile stock markets and an element of sticker shock after years of rising values all hit demand in the past couple of years.

Still, there are signs demand could start picking up. And Brexit's behind this, too.

The drop in sterling [http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/computer-driven-hedge-funds-in-focus-after-sterlings-plunge-1475838822]against global currencies after the Brexit vote has increased demand among foreign buyers, property brokers said.

For buyers with dollars, or currencies pegged to the U.S. currency like those in Hong Kong and Singapore, central London is the most affordable it has been since 2012, two years before prices peaked, a LonRes report said.

Even buyers from continental Europe are getting relative bargains.

"Exchange rate fluctuations will, we expect, create far greater buying opportunities for prime central London property than changes in prices," the report said.

Outside of London, the picture is similar: The number of people renting is on the rise, while property prices are cooling due to shrinking demand. U.K. house-prices fell 0.1% in the past three months, mortgage lender Halifax said in its monthly report Friday.

But unlike luxury pads in central London, falling demand is down to stretched affordability.

Throughout the U.K., "a lengthy period where house prices have risen more rapidly than earnings has put pressure on affordability, therefore constraining demand," said Martin Ellis, housing economist at Halifax.

MANSION --- Private Properties
By Candace Taylor
913 words

The Wall Street Journal
