Real Estate
Land Scarce for Luxury Homes; A shortage of developed land is driving up lot prices in affluent areas like Beverly Hills, Southampton, Miami and Chicago.
By Gabrielle Paluch
434 words
27 July 2016
10:47
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The demand for land is driving up prices—especially for prime lots in affluent neighborhoods.

Median list prices for land in the country's top luxury markets were up 8.7% in the first half of the year compared with the same period last year, according to listings website Realtor.com. The South saw the steepest increases, with median asking prices up 13.33% from last year.

In the U.S. overall, the median list price for a lot in a high-end neighborhood was $635,000, according to Realtor.com. Beverly Hills, Calif., Southampton, N.Y., and downtown neighborhoods in Miami, and Chicago saw the greatest jump in median land prices in luxury markets, according to Realtor.com. (News Corp, which owns The Wall Street Journal, also owns Realtor.com, the listing website of the National Association of Realtors.)

Driving up prices is a shortage of vacant, developed lots in prime areas, according to a survey by the National Association of Home Builders, a trade group. Released in May, the survey found that 69% of the 333 home builders surveyed said "A" lots—the most desirable parcels because of their location—were in low or very low supply.

The dearth can be traced back to the real-estate collapse that started in 2007, says Robert Dietz, NAHB's chief economist. He says the finite supply of land and a lack of developer financing have contributed to the shortage.

"The legacy of the Great Recession was that banks weren't lending," Mr. Dietz says. "Even when the mortgage lending came back acquisition, development, and construction loans were tight for years." When funds dried up, builders stopped or slowed the pace of developing land for future construction.

On the Hawaiian island of Oahu [http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/paradise-hawaiian-style-in-oahu-development-1464108780], beachfront properties near the commercial center in Waikiki, as well as land in the Diamond Head neighborhood are the most desirable, says Rodney Kim, vice president of Atlas Construction, a home builder based in Honolulu. "You have to look for bigger lots that can be subdivided," Mr. Kim says. "Unless you go out into rural areas, you can't find vacant lots, but that's where zoning restrictions get more problematic."

Canada Housing Agency Steps Up Risk Warning for Vancouver; Joins Toronto as city with ‘strong evidence' of problematic conditions related to soaring home prices
By Judy McKinnon
458 words
27 July 2016
19:46
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Corrections & Amplifications

Toronto and Vancouver, the country's two hottest markets, saw benchmark home prices jump 16% and 32%, respectively, in June. An earlier version of this article reversed the respective percentage changes for the cities.

Canada's main housing agency on Wednesday said it has now found "strong evidence" of problematic conditions related to soaring home prices in the country's two hottest markets of Vancouver and Toronto.

Canada Mortgage and Housing Corp. raised its alarm for Vancouver from its earlier finding of "moderate evidence" of problematic conditions, bringing its assessment for the city's housing market in line with its view of Toronto conditions.

"In Vancouver, we now have sufficient evidence to raise our overall assessment of problematic conditions in the Vancouver housing market to high," the agency said in its latest quarterly assessment of the country's housing markets.

For the country as a whole, the agency said evidence of problematic conditions, such as overvaluation and the threat of price gains, has increased to moderate from weak. It said overvaluation and overbuilding are still the main issues it sees across the 15 major markets it studies, noting it found overvaluation in nine cities and overbuilding in seven. Those levels are unchanged from its previous quarterly update in April.

The agency's red flag over conditions in Vancouver comes just two days after the provincial government in British Columbia unveiled a 15% property transfer tax[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/vancouver-unveils-new-tax-for-foreign-home-buyers-1469478909] for foreign home buyers in its biggest city in a bid to cool prices.

Soaring home prices in some Canadian cities in recent years, spurred on by ultralow interest rates, have had policy makers concerned about the risk of a possible bubble. Toronto and Vancouver, the country's two hottest markets, saw benchmark home prices jump 16% and 32%, respectively, in June—gains that the Bank of Canada has warned are likely unsustainable.

Along with low interest rates, Canadian housing officials have said foreign buyers are likely contributing to house-price increases, especially in Vancouver where Chinese buyers have been investing in high-end homes. Canadian Prime Minister Justin Trudeau said last month that housing affordability in Vancouver has become a "significant crisis," while Finance Minister Bill Morneau has said officials are conducting a so-called deep dive into what could be driving up real-estate prices.

The housing agency said weak energy prices continue to affect housing markets in oil-focused provinces such as Alberta and Saskatchewan. In Calgary, Alberta, which has been hard hit by the drop in crude-oil prices, the agency said it continues to see moderate evidence of overvaluation due in part to weaker economic fundamentals.

Canada Mortgage and Housing Corp. considers its Housing Market Assessment an early warning system for housing-market issues.

Housing Prices and Rising Capital Income; The rise in the housing capital income in the U.S. reflects low interest rates, low price inflation, and housing constraints, a BIS researcher finds.
172 words
26 July 2016
11:11
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

July 2016

The long-run increase in the housing capital income in the U.S. is the result of a combination of factors, including low interest rates, low price inflation, and housing constraints in certain urban areas, finds Gianni La Cava of the Bank for International Settlements[http://www.bis.org.proxy.library.brocku.ca/publ/work572.pdf]. The author builds on research by economists Thomas Piketty and Matthew Rognlie, who have previously charted the share of aggregate income going to capital in the U.S., and the fact that most of that income comes from the housing sector. The author writes that the majority of income going to owner-occupiers from housing capital is the result of the long-term decline in interest rates, particularly since the early 1980s, combined with a rising demand for housing that pushed up housing prices and rents relative to nonhousing prices.

--Gabriel T. Rubin
